    "body": "<div class=\"govspeak\"><h2 id=\"executive-summary\">\n<span class=\"number\">1. </span>Executive summary</h2>\n\n<p>This is a Budget that puts security first. It ensures economic security for working people by putting the public finances in order and setting out a bold plan for a more productive, balanced economy. It supports national security by investment in defence. It sets out bold reforms on tax and welfare, and introduces a National Living Wage so we move Britain from a low wage, high tax, high welfare economy to a higher wage, lower tax, lower welfare economy. It delivers on the promises on which the government was elected.</p>\n\n<p>Since 2010, the government has pursued a long-term economic plan that has halved the deficit as a share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr>. For the first time since 2001-02, the national debt is falling in 2015‑16, meeting the target set out in 2010. The UK was the fastest growing <abbr title=\"A group of 7 major industrial nations (comprising: Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, UK and US)\">G7</abbr> economy in 2014, employment has reached record levels, and wages are rising above inflation.</p>\n\n<p>But the job is not yet done. At 4.9%, the deficit remains too high, and productivity remains too low. The economy is still too unbalanced, and more needs to be done to build up the nations and regions of the UK, and to close the productivity gap between the north and south. The welfare bill is too high, and the welfare system traps too many people in benefit dependency. And for too long, the government has addressed low pay by subsidising it through the tax credit system, instead of delivering lower business taxes and asking business to pay higher wages.</p>\n\n<p>This Budget sets out the action the government will take to: </p>\n\n<ul>\n  <li>eliminate the deficit and run an overall surplus to start paying down debt, while increasing spending on defence and the NHS</li>\n  <li>reward work and back aspiration, by introducing a new National Living Wage, cutting taxes so people can keep more of what they earn, and reforming the welfare system to make it more affordable and fair to the taxpayers who pay for it </li>\n  <li>back business and make the economy more productive, by cutting corporation tax and increasing the permanent level of the Annual Investment Allowance, and by undertaking major reform of funding for skills and infrastructure to ensure higher standards of living for everyone in the UK</li>\n  <li>secure a truly national recovery, by devolving powers and budgets to build a Northern Powerhouse, and create the right conditions for strong growth throughout the UK</li>\n</ul>\n\n<h3 id=\"fixing-the-public-finances-and-running-a-surplus\">\n<span class=\"number\">1.1 </span>Fixing the public finances and running a surplus</h3>\n\n<p>The government’s long-term economic plan has laid the foundations for a stronger economy, and the UK’s recovery is now well established. The labour market remains strong, and in the 3 months to April 2015, employment was around record levels at 31.1 million. </p>\n\n<p>Earnings growth is continuing to strengthen, with earnings up 2.7% over the year in the 3 months to April 2015. Living standards are forecast to be higher in 2015 than they were in 2010, and are expected to continue to grow over the forecast period.</p>\n\n<p>However, Britain is still running a 4.9% deficit, and debt is above 80% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr>. As seen in recent weeks, the risks from abroad, not least within the euro area, demonstrate the need to secure the UK’s economic recovery.</p>\n\n<p>The government will continue to reduce the deficit by around 1.1% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> a year on average, the same pace as over the last Parliament. This fiscal path means that the deficit falls smoothly, and that debt falls as a share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in every year of the Parliament. At the same time, the government will ensure that the UK spends 2% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> a year on defence for the rest of the decade, and that spending on the NHS in England increases by £10 billion per annum in real terms by 2020-21. As a result of this plan, a surplus will be achieved in 2019-20, and debt as a share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in that year is forecast to be lower than expected at March Budget 2015. New fiscal rules will cement this fiscal path, and ensure that governments run surpluses in normal times. </p>\n\n<p>To achieve the surplus in 2019-20 the government will undertake around £37 billion of further consolidation measures. This Budget sets out around £17 billion of measures that will reduce the deficit, including £12 billion by 2019-20 from welfare reform and £5 billion by 2019-20 from tackling tax avoidance and tax planning, evasion and compliance, and imbalances in the tax system. In the autumn, the government will set out plans to deliver the remaining £20 billion of consolidation measures required to achieve the surplus following a rigorous Spending Review process.</p>\n\n<h3 id=\"economic-forecast\">\n<span class=\"number\">1.2 </span>Economic forecast</h3>\n\n<p>The Office for Budget Responsibility (<abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr>) forecasts <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> growth of 2.4% in 2015, 2.3% in 2016, and 2.4% for the remainder of the forecast period. </p>\n\n<p>The <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> forecasts employment to be 31.2 million in 2015, rising each year to 32.1 million in 2020. </p>\n\n<p><abbr title=\"Consumer Prices Index\">CPI</abbr> inflation is forecast to be below target in 2015, returning gradually to 2.0% in 2020.</p>\n\n<h3 id=\"fiscal-forecast\">\n<span class=\"number\">1.3 </span>Fiscal forecast</h3>\n\n<p>Public sector net borrowing is forecast to fall to 3.7% <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2015-16 and then to fall each year. The <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> forecasts that the public finances will return a surplus of £10 billion in 2019-20.</p>\n\n<p>Public sector net debt is forecast to peak at 80.8% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2014-19, before falling each year and reaching 68.5% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2020-21.</p>\n\n<h3 id=\"rewarding-work-and-supporting-aspiration\">\n<span class=\"number\">1.4 </span>Rewarding work and supporting aspiration</h3>\n\n<p>Underpinning the government’s approach is a commitment to reward work and support aspiration. This Budget ensures that the welfare system is fair to taxpayers while supporting the most vulnerable, and builds an economy based on higher pay, lower taxes and lower welfare. It ensures that the richest are paying a greater share of tax than they were at the start of the last Parliament, whilst the poorest continue to receive the bigger share of spending.</p>\n\n<p>This Budget:</p>\n\n<ul>\n  <li>introduces a new National Living Wage for workers aged 25 and above, and asks the Low Pay Commission to set out how it will reach 60% of median earnings by 2020; based on <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> forecasts, this means that the National Living Wage will reach the government’s target of over £9 by 2020</li>\n  <li>reduces taxes on working people by further increasing the personal allowance to £11,000 in 2016-17 and the higher rate threshold to £43,000; as a result of increases to the personal allowance announced since 2010, a typical basic rate taxpayer will be £905 better off</li>\n  <li>confirms that, from September 2017, free childcare entitlement will be doubled from 15 hours to 30 hours a week for working parents of 3 and 4 year olds</li>\n  <li>sets out the next stage of welfare reform, delivering on the government’s commitment to save £12 billion from the working-age welfare budget by 2019-20, including by freezing working-age benefits and Local Housing Allowances for 4 years, reducing rents in social housing by 1% a year for 4 years, reducing the benefit cap, and reforming tax credits and Universal Credit with support focussed on those with lower incomes </li>\n  <li>reforms the taxation of dividends by replacing the dividend tax credit with a Dividend Tax Allowance of £5,000 and setting new dividends tax rates</li>\n  <li>takes the family home out of inheritance tax for all but the wealthiest with a new transferable nil-rate band of up to £175,000 per estate when a main residence is passed to direct descendants; this means that the effective inheritance tax threshold will be £1 million</li>\n  <li>pays for this by introducing a taper to the annual allowance for pensions tax relief for those with total income over £150,000</li>\n  <li>increases the standard rate of insurance premium tax from 6% to 9.5%, and extends the time before a car needs its first MOT test from 3 years to 4 </li>\n  <li>restricts relief for mortgage interest for individual landlords to the basic rate of income tax, phased in over 4 years, limiting the advantage that these individuals currently enjoy over those purchasing their own home\nends the permanent non-domicile status </li>\n  <li>tackles tax evasion, avoidance and tax planning, increases tax compliance, and addresses imbalances in the tax system </li>\n</ul>\n\n<h3 id=\"backing-business-and-improving-productivity\">\n<span class=\"number\">1.5 </span>Backing business and improving productivity</h3>\n\n<p>The best way to create jobs and raise living standards over the long term is to support business and increase productivity by making it more competitive and by prioritising investment in skills and infrastructure. This \nBudget sets out a plan to back business and support productivity by:</p>\n\n<ul>\n  <li>cutting the corporation tax rate further to 19% in 2017 and 18% in 2020, benefitting over 1 million businesses, large and small</li>\n  <li>setting the level of the Annual Investment Allowance to £200,000 from January 2016, its highest ever permanent level</li>\n  <li>raising the Employment Allowance by £1,000 to £3,000 from April 2016 to support small businesses and charities to create jobs</li>\n  <li>introducing a levy on large employers to fund 3 million new, high quality apprenticeships this Parliament\nsupporting sustainable investment in universities by turning maintenance grants into loans, saving £2.5 billion by 2020-21</li>\n  <li>reforming vehicle excise duty for new cars and hypothecating the revenue from 2020 to a Roads Fund to ensure sustained investment in the strategic road network</li>\n</ul>\n\n<h3 id=\"ensuring-a-truly-national-recovery\">\n<span class=\"number\">1.6 </span>Ensuring a truly national recovery</h3>\n<p>The government believes that the only way to secure a truly national recovery is through a fundamental rebalancing of the British economy based on investment across the regions, growth driven by the private sector, and further devolution to increase local decision making. </p>\n\n<p>The government is: </p>\n\n<ul>\n  <li>delivering on its commitment to devolve further powers to Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland, introducing the Scotland Bill, and commencing discussions on a revised Scottish fiscal framework</li>\n  <li>building strong city regions by devolving further powers to Greater Manchester and working towards devolution deals with the Sheffield City Region, Liverpool City Region, and Leeds, West Yorkshire and partner authorities as part of building a Northern Powerhouse</li>\n  <li>supporting private-sector-led growth, and delivering on the long-term economic plans for the regions by continuing to invest in transport, science and skills  </li>\n</ul>\n\n<h3 id=\"budget-decisions\">\n<span class=\"number\">1.7 </span>Budget decisions</h3>\n\n<p>A summary of the fiscal impact of Budget policy decisions is set out in Table 1. Chapter 2 provides further information on the fiscal impact of the Budget.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"table-1-summary-of-budget-policy-decisions-1\">Table 1: Summary of Budget policy decisions (1)</h4>\n\n<table>\n  <thead>\n    <tr>\n      <th> </th>\n      <th> </th>\n      <th> </th>\n      <th>£ million</th>\n      <th> </th>\n      <th> </th>\n      <th> </th>\n    </tr>\n    <tr>\n      <th> </th>\n      <th>2015-16</th>\n      <th>2016-17</th>\n      <th>2017-18</th>\n      <th>2018-19</th>\n      <th>2019-20</th>\n      <th>2020-21</th>\n    </tr>\n  </thead>\n  <tbody>\n    <tr>\n      <td>Total spending policy decisions</td>\n      <td>+2,590</td>\n      <td>+5,095</td>\n      <td>+5,945</td>\n      <td>+8,270</td>\n      <td>+11,280</td>\n      <td>+12,415</td>\n    </tr>\n    <tr>\n      <td>Total tax policy decisions</td>\n      <td>+980</td>\n      <td>+3,980</td>\n      <td>+5,090</td>\n      <td>+6,825</td>\n      <td>+5,785</td>\n      <td>+6,470</td>\n    </tr>\n    <tr>\n      <td>TOTAL POLICY DECISIONS</td>\n      <td>+3,570</td>\n      <td>+9,075</td>\n      <td>+11,035</td>\n      <td>+15,095</td>\n      <td>+17,065</td>\n      <td>+18,885</td>\n    </tr>\n  </tbody>\n</table>\n\n<p>1 Costings reflect the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr>’s latest economic and fiscal determinants.</p>\n\n<h3 id=\"government-spending-and-revenue\">\n<span class=\"number\">1.8 </span>Government spending and revenue</h3>\n\n<p>Chart 1 shows public spending by main function. Total Managed Expenditure is expected to be around £742 billion in 2015-16.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"chart-1-public-sector-spending-2015-16\">Chart 1: Public sector spending 2015-16</h4>\n\n<figure class=\"image embedded\">\n  <div class=\"img\"><img alt=\"Chart 1: public sector spending 2015-16\" src=\"https://assets.digital.cabinet-office.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/image_data/file/41306/50325-Chart-1.jpg\"></div>\n</figure>\n\n<p>Chart 2 shows the different sources of government revenue. Public sector current receipts are expected to be around £673 billion in 2015-16.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"chart-2-public-sector-receipts-2015-16\">Chart 2: Public sector receipts 2015-16</h4>\n\n<figure class=\"image embedded\">\n  <div class=\"img\"><img alt=\"Chart 2: public sector receipts 2015-16\" src=\"https://assets.digital.cabinet-office.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/image_data/file/41307/50325-Chart-2.jpg\"></div>\n</figure>\n\n<h2 id=\"the-uk-economy-and-public-finances\">\n<span class=\"number\">2. </span>The UK economy and public finances</h2>\n\n<h3 id=\"uk-economy\">\n<span class=\"number\">2.1 </span>UK economy</h3>\n\n<p>The government’s long‑term economic plan has secured the recovery. The government’s fiscal responsibility has allowed monetary activism to support demand in the economy alongside repair of the financial sector. This has been supported by supply-side reform to deliver sustainable increases in standards of living. </p>\n\n<p>The UK’s economic recovery is well established. The UK was the fastest growing <abbr title=\"A group of 7 major industrial nations (comprising: Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, UK and US)\">G7</abbr> economy in 2014 growing by 3.0%, its best performance since 2006<sup id=\"fnref:1\"><a href=\"#fn:1\" class=\"footnote\">1</a></sup>. The Organisation for Economic Co‑operation and Development (<abbr title=\"Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development\">OECD</abbr>) forecasts the UK to be the fastest growing <abbr title=\"A group of 7 major industrial nations (comprising: Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, UK and US)\">G7</abbr> economy again in 2015, as shown in Chart 1.1.<sup id=\"fnref:2\"><a href=\"#fn:2\" class=\"footnote\">2</a></sup> Christine Lagarde, Managing Director of the International Monetary Fund (<abbr title=\"International Monetary Fund\">IMF</abbr>), said “when we look at the comparative growth rates delivered by various countries in Europe it’s obvious that what is happening in the U.K. has actually worked”.<sup id=\"fnref:3\"><a href=\"#fn:3\" class=\"footnote\">3</a></sup></p>\n\n<h4 id=\"chart-11-gdp-growth-in-the-g7\">Chart 1.1: <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> growth in the <abbr title=\"A group of 7 major industrial nations (comprising: Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, UK and US)\">G7</abbr>\n</h4>\n\n<figure class=\"image embedded\">\n  <div class=\"img\"><img alt=\"Chart 1.1\" src=\"https://assets.digital.cabinet-office.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/image_data/file/41308/50325-Chart-1.1.jpg\"></div>\n</figure>\n\n<p>The government’s long-term economic plan has laid solid foundations for a stronger economy. This Budget continues the work of repairing the public finances, addressing the long-standing weakness in productivity and rebalancing the economy. It recognises the risks from abroad and the need to secure Britain’s economic future. </p>\n\n<h3 id=\"employment-and-earnings\">\n<span class=\"number\">2.2 </span>Employment and earnings</h3>\n\n<h4 id=\"employment\">Employment</h4>\n\n<p>The UK labour market performance continues to be strong. In the 3 months to April the employment level and rate were both around record levels at 31.1 million and 73.4% respectively. The recent growth in employment predominantly reflects increases in full-time employment and those employed in high and medium-skilled occupations. Over the past year, 85% of the increase in employment has been in full-time work and 92% has been in high or medium-skilled jobs.<sup id=\"fnref:4\"><a href=\"#fn:4\" class=\"footnote\">4</a></sup></p>\n\n<p>The proportion of those who are inactive has been falling and is lower than it was before the crisis, but there are still too many working-age people who are not engaged in the labour force. Encouragingly, the increase in participation has been strong amongst women and older workers. The number of working-age women participating in the labour force has increased by over 700,000 since the beginning of the crisis. In the past year alone there have been an extra 200,000 workers aged over 50 in the labour force. </p>\n\n<p>The UK’s labour market has stood out among major advanced economies. Since the trough in the recession, the UK’s increase in employment rate has been the second largest in the <abbr title=\"A group of 7 major industrial nations (comprising: Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, UK and US)\">G7</abbr> behind only Germany.<sup id=\"fnref:5\"><a href=\"#fn:5\" class=\"footnote\">5</a></sup> The UK has taken another step towards achieving the government’s full-employment ambition to have the highest employment rate in the <abbr title=\"A group of 7 major industrial nations (comprising: Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, UK and US)\">G7</abbr>, having overtaken Canada in Q1 2015 to have the third highest employment rate in the <abbr title=\"A group of 7 major industrial nations (comprising: Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, UK and US)\">G7</abbr>, behind Japan and Germany, as shown in Chart 1.2.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"chart-12-international-comparison-of-employment-rates\">Chart 1.2: International comparison of employment rates</h4>\n\n<figure class=\"image embedded\">\n  <div class=\"img\"><img alt=\"Chart 1.2\" src=\"https://assets.digital.cabinet-office.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/image_data/file/41309/50325-Chart-1.2.jpg\"></div>\n</figure>\n\n<h4 id=\"earnings\">Earnings</h4>\n\n<p>Earnings growth is continuing to strengthen with earnings up 2.7% over the year in the 3 months to April, the fastest growth in real wages since 2007. The <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> forecasts earnings to continue to rise above inflation over the forecast period, as shown in Chart 1.3<sup id=\"fnref:6\"><a href=\"#fn:6\" class=\"footnote\">6</a></sup>. The low inflation recently experienced in the UK, driven by lower fuel and food costs, has helped support real incomes and household budgets. Compared to May 2014, fuel and food prices are 11.0% and 1.8% lower respectively.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"chart-13-earnings-growth-and-inflation\">Chart 1.3: Earnings growth and inflation</h4>\n\n<figure class=\"image embedded\">\n  <div class=\"img\"><img alt=\"Chart 1.3\" src=\"https://assets.digital.cabinet-office.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/image_data/file/41310/50325-Chart-1.3.jpg\"></div>\n</figure>\n\n<p>Real Household Disposable Income (<abbr title=\"Real Household Disposable Income\">RHDI</abbr>) per capita is the most up to date and comprehensive measure of living standards as it takes into account employment levels, the effects of tax and benefits, as well as inflation. Living standards, as measured by <abbr title=\"Real Household Disposable Income\">RHDI</abbr> per capita, are forecast to be higher in 2015 than they were in 2010 and to continue to grow over the forecast period. </p>\n\n<h3 id=\"the-uks-productivity-challenge\">\n<span class=\"number\">2.3 </span>The UK’s productivity challenge</h3>\n\n<p>A sustained improvement in productivity growth is critical to delivering the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr>’s forecast for the economy. It is also the single most important determinant of average living standards and is tightly linked to the differences in wages across countries.</p>\n\n<p>A large and long-standing productivity gap exists between the UK and other major advanced economies. Output per hour in the UK was 17 percentage points below the <abbr title=\"A group of 7 major industrial nations (comprising: Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, UK and US)\">G7</abbr> average, 27 percentage points below France, 28 percentage points below Germany and 31 percentage points below the US in 2013, as shown in Chart 1.4<sup id=\"fnref:7\"><a href=\"#fn:7\" class=\"footnote\">7</a></sup>. This gap existed prior to the financial crisis but, whilst the UK has not been alone in having weak productivity growth since the financial crisis and there are issues around the data, it is clear this gap has widened<sup id=\"fnref:8\"><a href=\"#fn:8\" class=\"footnote\">8</a></sup>. The recent weakness in productivity growth has also occurred alongside strong employment growth in the UK.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"chart-14-productivity-gap-with-the-uk-in-2013\">Chart 1.4: Productivity gap with the UK in 2013</h4>\n\n<figure class=\"image embedded\">\n  <div class=\"img\"><img alt=\"Chart 1.4\" src=\"https://assets.digital.cabinet-office.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/image_data/file/41311/50325-Chart-1.4.jpg\"></div>\n</figure>\n\n<p>The government is committed to reforming the UK economy to make it more productive. The government is publishing a productivity plan which tackles the UK’s serious long-term challenges, with major reforms to improve the UK’s infrastructure, tackle failures in the skills system, improve the planning system, encourage long-term finance for productive investment and give cities the governance and powers they need to succeed. Further information on the government’s plan to support productivity is set out below, in ‘Backing business and improving productivity’.</p>\n\n<h3 id=\"rebalancing-the-uk-economy\">\n<span class=\"number\">2.4 </span>Rebalancing the UK economy</h3>\n\n<h4 id=\"regional-rebalancing\">Regional rebalancing</h4>\n\n<p>London is one of the world’s greatest global cities and a huge asset for the national economy, but in recent decades the UK has relied too heavily on the capital to generate growth. The UK’s continued national prosperity depends on regions and cities outside the capital doing well. The government has a comprehensive plan to rebalance the economy and strengthen every part of the UK, bringing together the great cities and counties of the north of England, and supporting other vital regional economies such as the Midlands and South West. </p>\n\n<p>Since 2010 unemployment has fallen in every region and almost two-thirds of the UK-wide increase in private sector employment can be attributed to regions outside London and the South East. Output per head in the North West, North East, West Midlands and Wales grew faster than in London in 2013. The government will go further by supporting the resurgence of strong metro-wide areas through devolution, enabling cities to work together to take responsibility for their own economic success by the creation of an elected mayor. Further information on the government’s plan is set out below, in ‘Ensuring a truly national recovery’.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"sector-rebalancing\">Sector rebalancing</h4>\n\n<p>As the recovery has become established, growth has been more broadly balanced across sectors. There has been widespread growth across all major sectors since the start of 2013. Manufacturing, construction and services all grew by 3% or more in 2014, the first time since records began in 1990. </p>\n\n<p>After falling during the crisis, recent UK growth has been more investment rich with business investment increasing as share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr>. Real business investment has increased from 9.0% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2010 to 10.6% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2014 and is forecast to continue to do so. However, total investment as a share of output in 2014 was still lower in the UK than all other major advanced economies except Italy<sup id=\"fnref:9\"><a href=\"#fn:9\" class=\"footnote\">9</a></sup>. This is addressed in the productivity plan which will set out measures to encourage long-term investment in economic capital, including infrastructure, skills and knowledge.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"external-rebalancing\">External rebalancing</h4>\n\n<p>The UK is one of the most open economies in the world, with significant trade and financial links with other countries. Weak euro area growth has meant goods exports to <abbr title=\"European Union\">EU</abbr> countries have been subdued, falling by 5.3% since Q1 2008. However UK exports have continued to expand in other markets, as shown in Chart 1.5. The volume of goods exports to outside of the <abbr title=\"European Union\">EU</abbr> has increased by 24.1% since Q1 2008. The value of goods exports to the faster growing BRIC (Brazil, Russia, India, and China) economies has increased by 25.1% since Q1 2008.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"chart-15-trade-in-goods-since-2008\">Chart 1.5: Trade in goods since 2008</h4>\n\n<figure class=\"image embedded\">\n  <div class=\"img\"><img alt=\"Chart 1.5\" src=\"https://assets.digital.cabinet-office.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/image_data/file/41312/50325-Chart-1.5.jpg\"></div>\n</figure>\n\n<p>The UK’s trade balance as a share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> has improved slightly from -2.4% in 2010, to -2.0% in 2014 and is expected to improve further in 2015. The <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr>’s forecast for exports and imports over the forecast period reflects the slowdown in global trade volumes that has been seen since the early 2000s<sup id=\"fnref:10\"><a href=\"#fn:10\" class=\"footnote\">10</a></sup>. For any level of world <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr>, world trade is now expected to be lower and this feeds into a weaker outlook for UK trade, with other advanced economies also expecting to see lower exports in the next few years.<sup id=\"fnref:11\"><a href=\"#fn:11\" class=\"footnote\">11</a></sup></p>\n\n<p>The current account deficit was 5.9% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2014. The income balance component of the current account has declined since early 2012, reflecting lower income received from investment abroad. Weaker euro area growth and global prospects have seen UK investments abroad yield lower returns while, in contrast, as the UK economy has continued to recover, the payments made on foreign investments in the UK have increased. the authorities remain vigilant to any risks that may emerge.</p>\n\n<p>The UK’s large current account deficit contributes to the UK running the largest combined government and current account deficits in the <abbr title=\"A group of 7 major industrial nations (comprising: Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, UK and US)\">G7</abbr>, as shown in Chart 1.6. A current account deficit means the UK is a net foreign borrower and is acquiring more foreign liabilities than assets. The UK has run a persistent current account deficit for 30 years and a small deficit is sustainable with continued capital inflows. The UK’s budget deficit and debt position is in part financed by these capital inflows through purchases of UK government gilts. In comparison, countries that have higher levels of public debt but run small current account surpluses, such as Italy and Japan, are less exposed to financing risks from a shift in overseas invester sentiment. The government’s fiscal plan to complete the repair of the public finances should support a gradual narrowing of the current account deficit and reduce this exposure. </p>\n\n<h4 id=\"chart-16-combined-government-and-current-account-deficits-in-the-g7-in-2014\">Chart 1.6: Combined government and current account deficits in the <abbr title=\"A group of 7 major industrial nations (comprising: Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, UK and US)\">G7</abbr> in 2014</h4>\n\n<figure class=\"image embedded\">\n  <div class=\"img\"><img alt=\"Chart 1.6\" src=\"https://assets.digital.cabinet-office.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/image_data/file/41313/50325-Chart-1.6.jpg\"></div>\n</figure>\n\n<h4 id=\"housing-market-and-households\">Housing market and households</h4>\n\n<p>House price growth has moderated over the past year, having grown strongly during early 2014, with annual house price growth slowing to 5.5% in April 2015. Meanwhile, property transactions fell during the second half of 2014 and were 3.1% lower in May 2015 than a year earlier. The <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> forecasts house prices to grow by 5.7% in 2015, followed by 4.1% in 2016, before rising to 5.6% in 2020. Property transactions are forecast to fall by 1.7% in 2015 before growth picks-up, peaking at 5.5% in 2018.</p>\n\n<p>Household balance sheets have continued to normalise as households have reduced their debt as a proportion of income to 145% in Q1 2015, having peaked at 169% in Q1 2008. While households take on debt over the forecast period they also accumulate assets, meaning household net wealth as a proportion of income is forecast by the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> to increase from 8.3 times income in 2014 to 8.6 times income in 2020. </p>\n\n<h3 id=\"global-developments\">\n<span class=\"number\">2.5 </span>Global developments</h3>\n\n<p>The strength and sustainability of the global economic recovery is key to UK economic prospects. The global economic recovery remains uneven and the risks from the world economy, not least from within the euro area, demonstrate the need to continue to fix the economy to ensure the UK can deal with risks from abroad. </p>\n\n<p>In June 2015 the <abbr title=\"Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development\">OECD</abbr> revised down its global growth forecast for 2015 from 3.7% (forecast in November 2014) to 3.1%, with the global recovery gaining momentum but slowly<sup id=\"fnref:12\"><a href=\"#fn:12\" class=\"footnote\">12</a></sup>. The <abbr title=\"Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development\">OECD</abbr> revised down its forecast for US growth in 2015 from 3.1%, forecast in its March interim forecast, to 2.0%, while the euro area growth forecast was left unchanged at 1.4% in 2015. China’s growth is expected to moderate and to fall below 7% in 2015 to 6.8%. </p>\n\n<p>The <abbr title=\"International Monetary Fund\">IMF</abbr> last updated its economic forecast in April 2015 and forecast global growth at 3.5% in 2015<sup id=\"fnref:13\"><a href=\"#fn:13\" class=\"footnote\">13</a></sup>. The <abbr title=\"International Monetary Fund\">IMF</abbr>’s forecast included a marked shift in prospects across countries, with upward revisions in the euro area, Japan and India, and downward revisions for some emerging markets and the US. The <abbr title=\"International Monetary Fund\">IMF</abbr> will publish its revised global growth forecast on 9 July and the forecast will reflect developments over Q1 2015. Euro area growth in Q1 2015 was 0.4%, though the recovery remains vulnerable<sup id=\"fnref:14\"><a href=\"#fn:14\" class=\"footnote\">14</a></sup>. The US 2015 growth forecast was revised down in the <abbr title=\"International Monetary Fund\">IMF</abbr>’s June Article IV review to 2.5%, in light of weak Q1 2015 data.<sup id=\"fnref:15\"><a href=\"#fn:15\" class=\"footnote\">15</a></sup></p>\n\n<p>A number of other downside risks to the global recovery remain, including:</p>\n\n<ul>\n  <li>the clear risk that the ongoing situation in Greece spills over, causing renewed instability</li>\n  <li>the risk of renewed weakness in growth and inflation in the euro area </li>\n  <li>the risks posed to emerging markets with weak fundamentals from US rate rises and dollar appreciation </li>\n  <li>an escalation of geopolitical risks</li>\n  <li>the challenge for China in undertaking reform while maintaining financial stability</li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>The financial crisis in Greece is the biggest single external risk to the UK economy. It is vital that the current uncertainty is resolved, to ensure economic and financial stability across Europe. Britain will be more affected the longer the Greek crisis lasts, and the worse it gets. The government will do whatever is necessary to protect the UK’s economic security at this uncertain time. </p>\n\n<p>To deliver prosperity and security for all, the <abbr title=\"European Union\">EU</abbr> needs to be dynamic and outward focused, putting its resources to the most effective use. It must empower businesses to compete more effectively internationally by accelerating the integration of the single market, especially in services, and the financial, digital and energy sectors. Part of this would be the strengthening of the regulatory framework for business, improving the quality of regulation, and reducing excessive regulatory burdens on business. The <abbr title=\"European Union\">EU</abbr> also needs to be open to international trade and complete free-trade agreements with the US, Japan, and other developed economies, whilst also looking towards important new trading partners in Asia and South America.</p>\n\n<p>The government has a clear plan of reform, renegotiation and referendum, to make the <abbr title=\"European Union\">EU</abbr> a source of growth, jobs, innovation and success. The <abbr title=\"European Union\">EU</abbr> must make sure the interests of both those inside and outside the euro area are fairly balanced. The single currency is not for all member states in the <abbr title=\"European Union\">EU</abbr>, but the single market and the <abbr title=\"European Union\">EU</abbr> as a whole must work for all. </p>\n\n<p>The UK has joined the Asian Infrastructure Investment Bank (<abbr title=\"Asian Infrastructure Investment Bank\">AIIB</abbr>) as a founding member, following the announcement in March that the UK would be the first <abbr title=\"A group of 7 major industrial nations (comprising: Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan, UK and US)\">G7</abbr> member to apply to join. The UK’s involvement from the outset will help ensure that the <abbr title=\"Asian Infrastructure Investment Bank\">AIIB</abbr> embodies the best standards in accountability, transparency and governance, and help maximise the opportunities for British businesses and British jobs.</p>\n\n<h3 id=\"economic-outlook\">\n<span class=\"number\">2.6 </span>Economic outlook</h3>\n\n<h4 id=\"table-11-summary-of-the-obrs-central-economic-forecast-1\">Table 1.1: Summary of the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr>’s central economic forecast (1)</h4>\n\n<table>\n This is not intended to convey a degree of unwarranted accuracy. Components may not sum to total due to rounding and the statistical discrepancy.\n2 Includes households and non-profit institutions serving households.\n3 Includes transfer costs of non-produced assets.\n4 Contribution to <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> growth, percentage points.\n5 International Labour Organization.\nSource: Office for Budget Responsibility, Office for National Statistics.</p>\n\n<p>The <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> forecasts <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> growth of 2.4% in 2015 and 2.3% in 2016. Growth is revised up in 2017 and 2018 from 2.3% to 2.4% in both years and is unchanged in 2019 at 2.4%. The output gap represents the amount of spare capacity in the economy. </p>\n\n<p>The <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> forecasts the remaining spare capacity in the economy to be used up by mid-2018, half a year later than forecast in March 2015.</p>\n\n<p>The <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> forecasts employment to be 31.2 million in 2015, rising each year to 32.1 million in 2020. Unemployment is forecast to be 5.4% in 2015, falling to 5.1% in 2016 and 5.2% in 2017. Thereafter, unemployment is forecast to be 5.3% in 2018 and 5.4% in 2019 and 2020. </p>\n\n<p>The <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> has revised up business investment growth in 2015 to 6.0%. Business investment is forecast to grow by 7.2% in 2016 and by 6.5% or more in 2017, 2018 and 2019. The <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> expects private dwelling investment to grow by 6.3% in 2015 and 4.8% in 2016. Exports are forecast to grow by 3.8% in 2015 and 2016. Export growth over the forecast period is offset by import growth. </p>\n\n<p><abbr title=\"Consumer Prices Index\">CPI</abbr> inflation is forecast to be below target in 2015 and remain below the 2% inflation target before returning gradually to 2.0% in 2020.</p>\n\n<h3 id=\"monetary-policy\">\n<span class=\"number\">2.7 </span>Monetary policy</h3>\n\n<p>Monetary policy has a critical role to play in supporting the economy as the government delivers on its commitment for necessary fiscal consolidation, a policy strategy which has served the UK well since 2010. The government has ensured that monetary policy can continue to play that role fully by updating the UK’s monetary policy framework and remit for the Monetary Policy Committee (<abbr title=\"Monetary Policy Committee\">MPC</abbr>) at Budget 2013. The <abbr title=\"Monetary Policy Committee\">MPC</abbr> has full operational independence to set policy to meet the 2% inflation target as measured by the 12-month increase in the <abbr title=\"Consumer Prices Index\">CPI</abbr>.</p>\n\n<p>Inflation was 0.0% in March, triggering the second open letter for inflation falling more than 1 percentage point below target, published on 13 May 2015<sup id=\"fnref:16\"><a href=\"#fn:16\" class=\"footnote\">16</a></sup>. The low inflation recently experienced in the UK has mostly been driven by global factors, notably the sharp fall in oil prices and the decline in food prices during the second half of 2014. The Governor of the Bank of England’s open letter to the Chancellor explained that lower contributions from movements in food, energy and other goods prices accounted for around three-quarters of the shortfall in inflation from target in March.</p>\n\n<p>Inflation was -0.1% in April and 0.1% in May 2015. The <abbr title=\"Monetary Policy Committee\">MPC</abbr>’s view is that inflation is likely to remain close to its current rate over the next few months. At the time of the last open letter, the <abbr title=\"Monetary Policy Committee\">MPC</abbr> judged it likely that, conditional on interest rates following the path currently implied by market yields, slack in the economy will be absorbed and inflation will return to the 2% target within 2 years<sup id=\"fnref:17\"><a href=\"#fn:17\" class=\"footnote\">17</a></sup>.</p>\n\n<p>The Governor of the Bank of England has been clear that “a temporary period of falling prices, driven by large one-off adjustments in a few specific components of the <abbr title=\"Consumer Prices Index\">CPI</abbr> should not be mistaken for the potentially damaging process of ‘deflation’”<sup id=\"fnref:18\"><a href=\"#fn:18\" class=\"footnote\">18</a></sup>. The Chancellor has welcomed that the <abbr title=\"Monetary Policy Committee\">MPC</abbr> remains vigilant to both upside and downside risks to its forecast and stands ready to act if these risks materialise, to ensure inflation remains likely to return to target in a timely fashion.<sup id=\"fnref:19\"><a href=\"#fn:19\" class=\"footnote\">19</a></sup></p>\n\n<h4 id=\"credit-easing\">Credit easing</h4>\n\n<p>Credit conditions for businesses have continued to improve, supported by policy action the government has taken, such as through the Funding for Lending Scheme (<abbr title=\"Funding for Lending Scheme\">FLS</abbr>) and the British Business Bank. Gross lending to businesses has increased by 18% in the 5 months to May 2015 compared to the same period last year<sup id=\"fnref:20\"><a href=\"#fn:20\" class=\"footnote\">20</a></sup>. Gross lending to small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) has grown by a similar amount, while net lending to SMEs is also positive so far in 2015, having increased by £0.5 billion since January 2015<sup id=\"fnref:21\"><a href=\"#fn:21\" class=\"footnote\">21</a></sup>. Survey evidence also continues to point towards improving conditions. In response to the Federation of Small Businesses Voice of Small Business Survey, more SMEs reported that credit was affordable and available than at any time since the beginning of 2012<sup id=\"fnref:22\"><a href=\"#fn:22\" class=\"footnote\">22</a></sup>.</p>\n\n<p>The <abbr title=\"Funding for Lending Scheme\">FLS</abbr> will continue to provide support for bank lending to SMEs into 2016. Since its introduction in July 2012, the scheme has contributed to a significant fall in bank funding costs and helped support the improvement in credit conditions. Net lending to SMEs by participants in the scheme was £0.6 billion in Q1 2015<sup id=\"fnref:23\"><a href=\"#fn:23\" class=\"footnote\">23</a></sup>. This was higher than the quarterly average of -£0.3 billion over 2014 and consistent with the improvement in lending across the wider market<sup id=\"fnref:24\"><a href=\"#fn:24\" class=\"footnote\">24</a></sup>.</p>\n\n<h3 id=\"the-governments-fiscal-plan\">\n<span class=\"number\">2.8 </span>The government’s fiscal plan</h3>\n\n<p>Since 2010, the government has made significant progress on reducing the deficit and repairing the public finances. Over the course of the last Parliament, the deficit was more than halved as a percentage of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> from its post-war peak of 10.2% to reach 4.9% in 2014-15<sup id=\"fnref:25\"><a href=\"#fn:25\" class=\"footnote\">25</a></sup>. Debt is forecast to have peaked as a share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> at the end of 2014-15<sup id=\"fnref:26\"><a href=\"#fn:26\" class=\"footnote\">26</a></sup>. However, risks remain to the recovery, including from events in Greece and a slowing global economy. Debt stands at its highest share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> since the late 1960s, and the deficit remains among the highest in advanced economies.<sup id=\"fnref:27\"><a href=\"#fn:27\" class=\"footnote\">27</a></sup></p>\n\n<p>The government is taking further action to complete the repair of the public finances. Running a surplus on the headline measure of borrowing is the most reliable way to bring down debt as a share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in the long term.</p>\n\n<p>In the last Parliament, the headline measure of public sector net borrowing (<abbr title=\"Public Sector Net Borrowing\">PSNB</abbr>) was reduced by around 1.1% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> a year on average<sup id=\"fnref:28\"><a href=\"#fn:28\" class=\"footnote\">28</a></sup>. The government has decided to maintain the same average pace of reduction in the headline measure of <abbr title=\"Public Sector Net Borrowing\">PSNB</abbr> in this Parliament to reach an overall surplus in 2019-20.</p>\n\n<p>Table 1.2 sets out the key fiscal aggregates at the Budget. Public sector net debt (<abbr title=\"Public Sector Net Debt\">PSND</abbr>) as a share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> is forecast to fall in every year of the Parliament. Debt as a share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2019-20 is now forecast to be lower than expected in the March Budget. </p>\n\n<h4 id=\"table-12-comparison-of-key-fiscal-aggregates-to-march-budget-2015\">Table 1.2: Comparison of key fiscal aggregates to March Budget 2015</h4>\n\n<table>\n  <tbody>\n    <tr>\n      <td> </td>\n Therefore, the figure differs from the June Public Sector Finances release.\nSource: Office for Budget Responsibility.</p>\n\n<p>Tables 1.3 and 1.4 set out the changes between the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr>’s March 2015 forecast and Summer Budget 2015 forecast for <abbr title=\"Public Sector Net Borrowing\">PSNB</abbr> and <abbr title=\"Public Sector Net Debt\">PSND</abbr>. This shows that, following the in-year savings that the government announced in June and higher tax receipts, borrowing is forecast to be £6 billion lower in 2015-16. Increased asset sales strengthen the fiscal position over the forecast period, lowering the path of debt. This has allowed the government to increase departmental spending and smooth the path of borrowing over the Parliament, while delivering a larger surplus in 2019-20 than expected in March. Borrowing will be higher in the years 2016-17 to 2018-19 by an average of £9 billion a year, but the surplus of £10 billion in 2019-20 will be £3 billion higher than was forecast in March. Debt as a share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> will be lower in 2019-20 than forecast in March.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"table-13-changes-to-public-sector-net-borrowing-since-march-budget-2015\">Table 1.3: Changes to public sector net borrowing since March Budget 2015</h4>\n\n<table>\n  <thead>\n    <tr>\n Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr>.\nSource: Office for Budget Responsibility and HM Treasury calculations.</p>\n\n<p>To achieve the surplus in 2019-20 the government will undertake around £37 billion of further consolidation measures. As shown in Table 1.5, this Budget sets out around £17 billion of measures that will reduce the deficit, including £12 billion by 2019-20 from welfare reform and £5 billion by 2019-20 from tackling tax avoidance and tax planning, evasion and non-compliance, and imbalances in the tax system. In the autumn, the government will set out plans to deliver the remaining £20 billion of consolidation measures required to achieve the surplus following a rigorous Spending Review process.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"table-15-consolidation-plans-over-this-parliament\">Table 1.5: Consolidation plans over this Parliament</h4>\n\n<table>\n  <thead>\n    <tr>\n      <th> </th>\n      <th>£ billion</th>\n      <th> </th>\n      <th> </th>\n      <th> </th>\n    </tr>\n  </thead>\n  <tbody>\n    <tr>\n      <td> </td>\n      <td>2016-17</td>\n      <td>2017-18</td>\n      <td>2018-19</td>\n      <td>2019-20</td>\n    </tr>\n    <tr>\n      <td>Discretionary consolidation (1)</td>\n      <td>9</td>\n      <td>20</td>\n      <td>31</td>\n      <td>37</td>\n    </tr>\n    <tr>\n      <td>- of which announced at this Budget (2)</td>\n      <td>6</td>\n      <td>9</td>\n      <td>13</td>\n      <td>17</td>\n    </tr>\n    <tr>\n      <td>- -of which welfare reform</td>\n      <td>5</td>\n      <td>7</td>\n      <td>9</td>\n      <td>12</td>\n    </tr>\n    <tr>\n      <td>- - of which tax avoidance and tax planning, evasion and compliance, and imbalances in the tax system</td>\n      <td>1</td>\n      <td>2</td>\n      <td>4</td>\n      <td>5</td>\n    </tr>\n    <tr>\n      <td>Further consolidation</td>\n      <td>3</td>\n      <td>11</td>\n      <td>18</td>\n      <td>20</td>\n    </tr>\n  </tbody>\n</table>\n\n<p>1 Discretionary consolidation is calculated as the sum of: receipts from avoidance and tax planning, evasion and compliance and imbalances in the tax system and welfare policy decisions announced at Summer Budget 2015 and set out in Table 2.1; and the additional reduction in spending (or equivalent increase in taxes) needed to meet the government’s overall fiscal path, compared to a counterfactual in which <abbr title=\"Departmental Resource Expenditure Limits\">RDEL</abbr> excluding depreciation grows in line with the whole economy inflation from its 2015-16 level (excluding the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr>’s Allowance for Shortfall) and all other spending (and receipts) follows the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr>’s latest fiscal forecast.\n2 Total welfare policy decisions and total receipts from avoidance and tax planning, evasion and compliance, and imbalances in the tax system as set out in Table 2.1.\nSource: Office for Budget Responsibility, HM Treasury policy costings and HM Treasury calculations.</p>\n\n<p>The Spending Review will identify further reductions, while confirming increased spending on the NHS and ensuring the UK spends 2% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> a year on defence. The government will also look to do more to tackle avoidance and tax planning, evasion and compliance, and imbalances in the tax system, and will continue to consider sensible welfare reform.</p>\n\n<h3 id=\"the-long-term-debt-challenge\">\n<span class=\"number\">2.9 </span>The long-term debt challenge</h3>\n<p>High debt means a high burden of interest costs on future generations. Even with interest rates at historically low levels, central government gross debt interest spending equates to the budgets of the Ministry of Defence and Home Office combined in 2015-16, as set out in Table 2.3<sup id=\"fnref:29\"><a href=\"#fn:29\" class=\"footnote\">29</a></sup>. Reducing debt from its current high levels will also help prepare the UK to deal with future pressures from an ageing population.</p>\n\n<p>High debt increases the UK’s vulnerability to future shocks. Evidence suggests that at higher debt levels, the scope for fiscal policy to stabilise the economy is reduced<sup id=\"fnref:30\"><a href=\"#fn:30\" class=\"footnote\">30</a></sup>. A higher starting level of debt also increases the risk that a further shock to the public finances could increase debt to a level that the markets would view as potentially unsustainable, increasing interest rates.</p>\n\n<p>The evidence suggests that negative effects of debt are likely to start to dominate with gross debt in the region of 70-90% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr>. Without further debt reduction, a future economic shock on the scale of the Great Recession of 2008 would take debt to well over 100% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr><sup id=\"fnref:31\"><a href=\"#fn:31\" class=\"footnote\">31</a></sup>. This risk can be exacerbated by country-specific factors, such as potential vulnerabilities from the UK’s large current account deficit, large financial centre, and any negative feedback of high debt on growth<sup id=\"fnref:32\"><a href=\"#fn:32\" class=\"footnote\">32</a></sup>.</p>\n\n<p>A strategy for debt reduction must take into account the possibility of future economic shocks. The UK economy has been subject to relatively frequent shocks in the past and though the nature and timing are unpredictable, prudent fiscal policy should allow for them. Any analysis is necessarily illustrative, but Chart 1.7 below shows the impact on debt by 2035-36 of a simple scenario in which the economy is hit once every 8 years by a shock that increases <abbr title=\"Public Sector Net Debt\">PSND</abbr> by 10% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr><sup id=\"fnref:33\"><a href=\"#fn:33\" class=\"footnote\">33</a></sup>. In this illustrative scenario, running a permanent 1.4% deficit (equivalent to a balanced current budget) after 2020-21 means debt only falls by 8% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> between its peak in 2014-15 and 2035-36. In comparison, continuing to run an overall surplus of 0.5% beyond the forecast period reduces debt by 28% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr>.</p>\n\n<h4 id=\"chart-17-public-sector-net-debt-in-2014-15-and-hm-treasury-projections-in-2035-36-under-different-policy-assumptions-with-and-without-illustrative-shocks\">Chart 1.7: Public sector net debt in 2014-15 and HM Treasury projections in 2035-36 under different policy assumptions with and without illustrative shocks</h4>\n\n<figure class=\"image embedded\">\n  <div class=\"img\"><img alt=\"Chart 1.7\" src=\"https://assets.digital.cabinet-office.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/image_data/file/41314/50325-Chart-1.7.jpg\"></div>\n</figure>\n\n<p>Any strategy for long-term debt reduction must also take into account the UK’s low inflation environment. Independent monetary policy now delivers low and stable medium-term inflation, to the benefit of the whole economy. This contrasts with the experience after World War II, when very high inflation, together with financial repression, played a major role in reducing debt<sup id=\"fnref:34\"><a href=\"#fn:34\" class=\"footnote\">34</a></sup>. Substantial debt reduction in future will depend on responsible management of the public finances and sustainable economic growth.</p>\n\n<p>The only reliable way to bring debt down to safer levels is to run a surplus in normal times<sup id=\"fnref:35\"><a href=\"#fn:35\" class=\"footnote\">35</a></sup>. Running a surplus will mean that debt falls rapidly when the economy is growing normally – ensuring that long-term debt reduction will not be knocked off-course by periodic shocks.</p>\n\n<h3 id=\"charter-for-budget-responsibility\">\n<span class=\"number\">2.10 </span>Charter for Budget Responsibility</h3>\n\n<p>The government has published a draft Charter for Budget Responsibility (the “Charter”) to entrench this commitment to reach an overall surplus and maintain it in normal times. The draft Charter sets out:</p>\n\n<ul>\n  <li>a target for a surplus on public sector net borrowing in 2019-20, and a supplementary target for public sector net debt to fall as a share of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in each year from 2015-16 to 2019-20</li>\n  <li>a target, once a surplus is achieved in 2019-20, to run a surplus each subsequent year as long as the economy remains in normal times</li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>These targets will apply as long as the economy is not hit by a significant negative shock that reduces real <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> growth to less than 1% (on a rolling 4 quarter-on-4 quarter basis). If the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> judge that the economy has been hit by a shock, the surplus rule will be suspended. This will allow the automatic stabilisers to support the economy when they are needed. The framework therefore supports fiscal discipline in normal times, while ensuring that future governments will have the flexibility to respond appropriately to shocks.</p>\n\n<p>Following a shock, the government of the day will be required to set a plan to return to surplus. This plan must include appropriate fiscal targets. The framework does not prescribe what the targets should be, allowing the government of the day to respond to the circumstances. However, the targets will be voted on by the House of Commons and assessed by the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr>. A surplus in normal times is necessary to provide the government of the day with the fiscal space to allow appropriate action to be taken in the face of these shocks. The end goal must be to return the public finances to surplus, ensuring that long-term debt reduction continues.</p>\n\n<p>The Charter will be laid before Parliament and voted on by the House of Commons in the autumn of 2015.</p>\n\n<h3 id=\"the-obrs-fiscal-forecast\">\n<span class=\"number\">2.11 </span>The <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr>’s fiscal forecast</h3>\n\n<p>As a result of the government’s plan, the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> is forecasting that the public finances will return to surplus in 2019-20. The surplus in 2020-21 would be the largest structural surplus in 40 years.</p>\n\n<p>Table 1.4 sets out the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr> forecast for key fiscal aggregates over this Parliament, based on the strategy defined in this Budget.</p>\n\n<p>From its post-war peak of 10.2% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2009-10<sup id=\"fnref:36\"><a href=\"#fn:36\" class=\"footnote\">36</a></sup>, <abbr title=\"Public Sector Net Borrowing\">PSNB</abbr> is forecast to fall to:</p>\n\n<ul>\n  <li>3.7% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2015-16</li>\n  <li>a surplus of 0.4% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2019-20 and 0.5% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2020-21</li>\n</ul>\n\n<p><abbr title=\"Public Sector Net Debt\">PSND</abbr> is forecast to peak at 80.8% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2014-15, before falling each year and reaching 68.5% of <abbr title=\"Gross Domestic Product\">GDP</abbr> in 2020-21. </p>\n\n<p>Table 1.6: Overview of the <abbr title=\"Office for Budget Responsibility\">OBR</abbr>’s central fiscal (5)</td>\n      <td> </td>\n      <td>0.2</td>\n      <td>0.5</td>\n      <td>0.5</td>\n      <td>0.7</td>\n      <td>0.8</td>\n      <td>0.8</td>\n    </tr>\n  </tbody>\n</table>\n\n<p>1 General
